</div></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Politics_of_England" title="Template:Politics of England"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Politics_of_England" title="Template talk:Politics of England"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Politics_of_England&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>North East England</b> is one of nine official <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_England" title="Regions of England">regions of England</a> at the <a href="/wiki/First-level_NUTS_of_the_European_Union" title="First-level NUTS of the European Union">first level</a> of <a href="/wiki/NUTS_statistical_regions_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="NUTS statistical regions of the United Kingdom">NUTS</a> for <a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">statistical</a> purposes. It covers <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Durham" title="County Durham">County Durham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyne_and_Wear" title="Tyne and Wear">Tyne and Wear</a>, and the former county of <a href="/wiki/Cleveland,_England" title="Cleveland, England">Cleveland</a> now in <a href="/wiki/North_Yorkshire" title="North Yorkshire">North Yorkshire</a>. The region is home to three large <a href="/wiki/Conurbation" title="Conurbation">conurbations</a>: <a href="/wiki/Teesside" title="Teesside">Teesside</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wearside" title="Wearside">Wearside</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tyneside" title="Tyneside">Tyneside</a>, the last of which is the largest of the three and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_conurbations_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of conurbations in the United Kingdom">the eighth most populous conurbation in the United Kingdom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> There are three cities in the region: <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a>, the largest, with a population of just under 280,000; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunderland,_Tyne_and_Wear" title="Sunderland, Tyne and Wear">Sunderland</a>, also in the metropolitan county of Tyne and Wear; and <a href="/wiki/Durham,_England" title="Durham, England">Durham</a>. Other large towns include <a href="/wiki/Darlington" title="Darlington">Darlington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gateshead" title="Gateshead">Gateshead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hartlepool" title="Hartlepool">Hartlepool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough" title="Middlesbrough">Middlesbrough</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Shields" title="South Shields">South Shields</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stockton-on-Tees" title="Stockton-on-Tees">Stockton-on-Tees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington,_Tyne_and_Wear" title="Washington, Tyne and Wear">Washington</a>. 
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_early_history">Geography and early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography and early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Geographic features of the North East</div><div class="tsingle" class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Castle" title="Lindisfarne Castle">Lindisfarne Castle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">Holy Island</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:194px;max-width:194px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:144px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/High_Force_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1295229.jpg/288px-High_Force_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1295229.jpg 1.5x, style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/High_Force" title="High Force">High Force</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teesdale" title="Teesdale">Teesdale</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:194px;max-width:194px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:144px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Summit_of_Windy_Gyle_-_geograph.org.uk_-_670034.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Summit_of_Windy_Gyle_-_geograph.org.uk_-_670034.jpg/192px-Summit_of_Windy_Gyle_-_geograph.org.uk_-_670034.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Summit_of_Windy_Gyle_-_geograph.org.uk_-_670034.jpg/288px-Summit_of_Windy_Gyle_-_geograph.org.uk_-_670034.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Summit_of_Windy_Gyle_-_geograph.org.uk_-_670034.jpg/384px-Summit_of_Windy_Gyle_-_geograph.org.uk_-_670034.jpg 2x" width="192"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Cheviot_Hills" title="Cheviot Hills">Cheviot Hills</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:194px;max-width:194px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:144px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whitley_Bay_Beach_-_geograph.org.uk_-_714360.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Whitley_Bay_Beach_-_geograph.org.uk_-_714360.jpg/192px-Whitley_Bay_Beach_-_geograph.org.uk_-_714360.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Whitley_Bay_Beach_-_geograph.org.uk_-_714360.jpg/288px-Whitley_Bay_Beach_-_geograph.org.uk_-_714360.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Whitley_Bay_Beach_-_geograph.org.uk_-_714360.jpg/384px-Whitley_Bay_Beach_-_geograph.org.uk_-_714360.jpg 2x" width="192"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Whitley_Bay" title="Whitley Bay">Whitley Bay</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>
<p>The region is generally hilly and sparsely populated in the North and West, and urban and arable in the East and South. The highest point in the region is <a href="/wiki/The_Cheviot" title="The Cheviot">The Cheviot</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Cheviot_Hills" title="Cheviot Hills">Cheviot Hills</a>, at 815 metres (2,674 ft).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Durham_St_Cuthbert.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="406" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Durham_St_Cuthbert.jpg/220px-Durham_St_Cuthbert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Durham_St_Cuthbert.jpg/330px-Durham_St_Cuthbert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a2/Durham_St_Cuthbert.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Durham_St_Cuthbert.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>12th-century wall-painting of St Cuthbert in Durham Cathedral</div></div></div>
<p>The region contains the urban centres of <a href="/wiki/Tyneside" title="Tyneside">Tyneside</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wearside" title="Wearside">Wearside</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teesside" title="Teesside">Teesside</a>, and is noted for the rich natural beauty of its coastline, <a href="/wiki/Northumberland_National_Park" title="Northumberland National Park">Northumberland National Park</a>, and the section of the <a href="/wiki/Pennines" title="Pennines">Pennines</a> that includes <a href="/wiki/Teesdale" title="Teesdale">Teesdale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weardale" title="Weardale">Weardale</a>. The regions historic importance is displayed by Northumberland's ancient castles, the two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Sites" title="World Heritage Sites">World Heritage Sites</a> of <a href="/wiki/Durham_Cathedral" title="Durham Cathedral">Durham Cathedral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Durham_Castle" title="Durham Castle">Durham Castle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian's Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> one of the frontiers of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. In fact, Roman archaeology can be found widely across the region and a special exhibition based around the Roman Fort of Segedunum at Wallsend<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and the other forts along Hadrian's Wall are complemented by the numerous artifacts that are displayed in the Great North Museum Hancock<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> in Newcastle. <a href="/wiki/St_Peter%27s_Church,_Monkwearmouth" title="St Peter's Church, Monkwearmouth">St. Peter's Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Monkwearmouth" title="Monkwearmouth">Monkwearmouth</a>, Sunderland and St. Pauls in <a href="/wiki/Jarrow" title="Jarrow">Jarrow</a> also hold significant historical value and have a joint bid to become a World Heritage Site.
</p><p>The area has a strong religious past, as can be seen in works such as the <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Gospels" title="Lindisfarne Gospels">Lindisfarne Gospels</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The work of the 7th-century <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert" title="Cuthbert">Cuthbert</a> (634–687 AD), <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> (673–735 AD) and <a href="/wiki/Hilda_of_Whitby" title="Hilda of Whitby">Hilda of Whitby</a> (614–680 AD) were hugely influential in the early church, and are still venerated by some today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> These saints are usually associated with the monasteries on the island of <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">Lindisfarne</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wearmouth-Jarrow" title="Wearmouth-Jarrow">Wearmouth – Jarrow</a>, and the Abbey at <a href="/wiki/Whitby" title="Whitby">Whitby</a>, though they are also associated with many other religious sites in the region. Bede is regarded as the greatest Anglo-Saxon scholar. He worked at the monasteries of Wearmouth and Jarrow, translating some forty books on all areas of knowledge, including nature, history, astronomy, poetry and theological matters such as the lives of the saints. His best known work is "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Ecclesiastical_History_of_the_English_People" title="The Ecclesiastical History of the English People">The Ecclesiastical History of the English People</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> One of the most famous pieces of art and literature created in the region is the <i><a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne_Gospels" title="Lindisfarne Gospels">Lindisfarne Gospels</a></i>, and are thought to be the work of a monk named Eadfrith, who became Bishop of Lindisfarne in 698. This body of work is thought to have been created in honour of Cuthbert, around 710–720.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 6 June 793 the Vikings arrived on the shores of north-east England with a raiding party from Norway who attacked the monastic settlement on Lindisfarne<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> . The monks fled or were slaughtered, and Bishop Higbald sought refuge on the mainland. A chronicler recorded: "On the 8th June, the harrying of the heathen miserably destroyed God's church by rapine and slaughter."  There were three hundred years of Viking raids, battles and settlement until <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> defeated King Harold at Hastings in 1066.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The <i>Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</i> notes the change from raiding to settlement when it records that in 876 the Vikings "Shared out the land of the Northumbrians and they proceeded to plough and support themselves"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The Anglo-Saxon Kingdom of Northumbria extended from the Scottish borders (then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pictish" title="Pictish">Pictish</a> borders) at the Firth of Forth to the north, and to the south of <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>, its capital, down to the Humber. The last independent Northumbrian king from 947–8 was <a href="/wiki/Eric_Bloodaxe" title="Eric Bloodaxe">Eric Bloodaxe</a>, who died at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stainmore" title="Battle of Stainmore">Battle of Stainmore</a>, Westmorland, in 954. After Eric Bloodaxe's death, all England was ruled by <a href="/wiki/Eadred" title="Eadred">Eadred</a>, the grandson of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a>; and so began the machinery of national government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Today the Viking legacy can still be found in the language and place names of north-east England and in the DNA of its people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The name <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle</a> comes from the castle built shortly after the conquest in 1080 by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curthose" title="Robert Curthose">Robert Curthose</a>, William the Conqueror's eldest son.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>North East England has an <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic climate</a> with narrower temperature ranges than the south of England. Summers and winters are mild rather than extremely hot or cold, due to the strong maritime influence of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Current</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Met_Office" title="Met Office">Met Office</a> operates several weather stations in the region<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and are able they show the regional variations in temperature and its relation to the distance from the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>. The warmest summers in the region are found in Stockton-on-Tees and the <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough" title="Middlesbrough">Middlesbrough</a> area, with a 1981-2010 July average high of 20.4 °C (68.7 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Precipitation is often low by English standards, in spite of the low levels of sunshine, with Stockton-on-Tees averaging only 574.2 millimetres (22.61 in) annually, and with the seaside town of Tynemouth (despite its slightly sunnier climate) recording 597.2 millimetres (23.51 in) annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The summers on the northern coastlines are significantly cooler than in the southern and central inland areas: Tynemouth is only just above 18 °C (64 °F) in July. Further inland, frosts during winter are more common, due to the higher elevations and distance from the sea.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_heritage">Industrial heritage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Industrial heritage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>After more than 2,000 years of industrial activity as a result of abundant minerals such as salt and coal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">chemical industry</a> of the Northeast England is today spread across the whole of the region<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceuticals" title="Pharmaceuticals">pharmaceuticals</a> being primarily produced in the north of the region, <a href="/wiki/Speciality_chemicals" title="Speciality chemicals">speciality</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fine_chemicals" title="Fine chemicals">fine chemicals</a> spread across the middle of the region and <a href="/wiki/Commodity_chemicals" title="Commodity chemicals">commodity chemicals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petrochemicals" title="Petrochemicals">petrochemicals</a> on Teesside. These companies are members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northeast_of_England_Process_Industry_Cluster" title="Northeast of England Process Industry Cluster">Northeast of England Process Industry Cluster</a> (NEPIC). The early chemical industry in this region, however, was primarily Tyneside based and associated with the manufacture of soap and glass. The most important chemical activity in the 18th and 19th centuries was the manufacture of alkali to make soap, which was when mixed with lime and sand and used to make glass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpson_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simpson-22">[22]</a></sup>  The effects of the industrial revolution could be seen through an economy dominated by iron and steel, coal mining and shipbuilding.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alkali_manufacturing">Alkali manufacturing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Alkali manufacturing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friars_Goose_Alkali_Works_Engraving.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="272" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Friars_Goose_Alkali_Works_Engraving.png/220px-Friars_Goose_Alkali_Works_Engraving.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Friars_Goose_Alkali_Works_Engraving.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friars_Goose_Alkali_Works_Engraving.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Friars Goose Alkali Works had the highest chimney in England to disperse hydrochloric acid fumes</div></div></div>
<p>Before the industrial revolution alkali was mostly used to aid the bleaching process of cloth. As the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> took hold, increasing demand for alkali came from higher production of dyestuffs, and bleach. In 1798 John Losh and the Earl of Dundonald took out a lease for a rich supply of brine pumped from a nearby coal mine, the Walker pit, becoming the supplier of raw material for The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Losh,_Wilson_%26_Bell" title="Losh, Wilson &amp; Bell">Losh, Wilson &amp; Bell</a> Alkali works. The works were established at Walker-on-Tyne in 1807 and bleaching powder manufacture began there in 1830, Losh Brothers soon manufactured half the soda in England. By 1814 the <a href="/wiki/Leblanc_process" title="Leblanc process">Leblanc process</a> of making alkali from common salt was introduced to Britain. Alkali works using this process opened at <a href="/wiki/Tyne_Dock" title="Tyne Dock">Tyne Dock</a> 1822, Felling shore <a href="/wiki/Tyneside" title="Tyneside">Tyneside</a> 1826, Friars Goose <a href="/wiki/Gateshead" title="Gateshead">Gateshead</a> 1828 and again on Felling Shore in 1834. Such works also produced soda, alum and Epsom salts. 
The river frontage at South Shore of the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">River Tyne</a> at Gateshead was one of the main locations for the chemical industry such that in the 19th century, which led to a cluster of iron, soap and alkali manufacturing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> By 1828 the alkali works  had large problem controlling emissions of <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> fumes which devastated the neighbouring countryside. One solution was to build tall chimneys to drive the fumes further away and in 1833 the tallest chimney in England was built at the Friars Goose Alkali Works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpson_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Simpson-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The passing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_Act" title="Alkali Act">Alkali Act</a> of 1863 in the UK Parliament brought about a further reduced pollution from these processes and was the first industrial environmental legislation to come into practice globally.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teesside_chemicals">Teesside chemicals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Teesside chemicals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Robert Wilson first produced sulphuric acid and fertilisers at Urlay Nook near Egglescliffe in 1833,  becoming <a href="/wiki/Teesside" title="Teesside">Teesside's</a> first great chemical works. In 1859 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_salt" title="Rock salt">rock salt</a> deposits were discovered in Middlesbrough by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bolckow" title="Henry Bolckow">Henry Bolckow</a> and Vaughan while boring for water and led to the move  of the heavy chemical industry to Teesside. In 1860 William James established an alkali company at Cargo Fleet and in 1869 <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Sadler" title="Samuel Sadler">Samuel Sadler</a> also set up a factory nearby. Sadler's works produced synthetic aniline and alzarine dyestuffs and distilled tar. The introduction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solvay_Process" title="Solvay Process">Solvay Process</a> to make alkali in 1872 made the Tyneside alkali industry uneconomic but it was a real boost to Teesside industry which was invigorated by the discovery of further salt deposits at Port Clarence near Seal Sands by Bell Brothers in 1874.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpson_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Simpson-22">[22]</a></sup> Salt extraction for human consumption had taken place at <a href="/wiki/Seal_Sands" title="Seal Sands">Seal Sands</a> since Roman times through the use of panning. This continued throughout the 20th century and left caverns which are now used as liquid/gas strorage facilities for the process industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Brunner_Mond_Company">The Brunner Mond Company</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Brunner Mond Company">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By 1882 a number of salt works were established at Haverton Hill in 1882 by the Bell Brothers. The company became the first firm to begin large scale salt production in Teesside, and necessitated that workers were brought in from Cheshire. The salt-making interests of Bell Brothers were bought by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brunner_Mond" title="Brunner Mond">Brunner Mond</a> &amp; Co of Cheshire in 1890. Brunner Mond became a giant of Teesside chemical-making in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. Meanwhile, rationalization of chemical firms in 1891 left only four works on Tyneside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpson_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Simpson-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemicals_at_Billingham">Chemicals at Billingham</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Chemicals at Billingham">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_Industry" title="Chemical Industry">Chemical Industry</a> was established at <a href="/wiki/Billingham" title="Billingham">Billingham</a> in 1918 by the Government for the production of synthetic <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, with its intended use being the manufacture of munitions during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_War" title="Great War">Great War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The 700 acre Grange Farm at Billingham was chosen for the site. However, by the time the plant opened the war was over and its manufacturing techniques outdated. It was taken over by Brunner Mond in 1920 and manufactured synthetic ammonia and fertilisers. Brunner Mond merged with other large scale chemical manufacturers in 1926 to form <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chemical_Industries" title="Imperial Chemical Industries">Imperial Chemical Industries</a> (ICI). In 1928 anhydrite was mined from below Billingham for use in the making of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphuric_acid" title="Sulphuric acid">sulphuric acid</a>, a necessary component for the manufacture of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detergents" title="Detergents">detergents</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilizers" title="Fertilizers">fertilizers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpson_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-Simpson-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plastics_and_nylon_manufacture">Plastics and nylon manufacture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Plastics and nylon manufacture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Manufacturing of plastics commenced at Billingham in 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> This was one of the earliest sites in the world where large-scale manufacture of these materials took place. Another <a href="/wiki/Chemical_plant" title="Chemical plant">chemical plant</a> was established the following year to make oil and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petrol" title="Petrol">petrol</a> from <a href="/wiki/Creosote" title="Creosote">creosote</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> by a process called <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a>. In 1946 another large chemical works opened on Teesside at <a href="/wiki/Wilton,_Redcar_and_Cleveland" title="Wilton, Redcar and Cleveland">Wilton</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> on the south side of the <a href="/wiki/River_Tees" title="River Tees">River Tees</a>. Further lands were purchased by ICI in 1962 at Seal Sands, where land had been reclaimed from the sea, becoming the third large-scale chemical manufacturing site on Teesside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpson_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-Simpson-22">[22]</a></sup> Today all three Teesside chemical sites at Billingham, Wilton, and Seal Sands remain in use for large-scale chemical manufacture by the members of the Northeast of England Process Industry Cluster (NEPIC), and plastics and polymers continue to be manufactured there by <a class="external text" href="http://www.lottechemical.co.uk" rel="nofollow">Lotte Chemicals</a>(<a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_terephthalate" title="Polyethylene terephthalate">PET</a>), <a href="/wiki/SABIC" title="SABIC">SABIC</a>(<a href="/wiki/Polyethylene" title="Polyethylene">HDPE</a>), <a href="/wiki/Victrex" title="Victrex">Victrex</a>(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PEEK" title="PEEK">PEEK</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucite" title="Lucite">Lucite International</a>(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perspex" title="Perspex">Perspex</a>). Nylon 66 manufacture ceased on Teesside in 2008 with the closure of the <a href="/wiki/Invista" title="Invista">Invista</a> manufacturing unit.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Petrochemical_production">Petrochemical production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Petrochemical production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coke_ovens" title="Coke ovens">Coke ovens</a> used in the production of chemicals at Billingham were replaced in 1962 by plants using the steam <a href="/wiki/Naphtha" title="Naphtha">naphtha</a> process, which enabled the use of crude oil as feedstock for the process known as cracking. This proved to be a much cheaper way to produce <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic_chemical_compound" title="Aromatic chemical compound">aromatics</a>, petroleum derivatives and other chemicals such as <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>. From 1964 to 1969 four large oil refineries were erected at the mouth of the Tees, two by Phillips Petroleum and one each by ICI and Shell. Their main purpose was to supply the Billingham chemical industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simpson_22-6"><a href="#cite_note-Simpson-22">[22]</a></sup> A 138-mile (222 km) pipeline was built in 1968 for the transport of ethylene and linked chemical works on Teesside with chemical plants at Runcorn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Today the remaining oil refinery is operated by ConocoPhillips and two biorefineries which produce <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioethanol" title="Bioethanol">bioethanol</a> as transport fuels, are operated by Ensus and Harvest Energy. <a href="/wiki/SABIC" title="SABIC">SABIC</a> operate the ethylene cracker and the aromatics plants, while the ammonia and fertiliser works are operated by CF Fertilisers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salt_making">Salt making</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Salt making">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Salt-making in and around <a href="/wiki/Greatham,_County_Durham" title="Greatham, County Durham">Greatham</a> (between Hartlepool and Billingham) had been important in Roman and medieval times,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and salt was also produced on Wearside from the 1580s, but by the 16th century the industry had been eclipsed by South Shields on the Tyne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1894 the industry returned to Greatham with the establishment of the Greatham Salt and Brine Company by George Weddell. The works was later purchased by the famous salt-making company <a href="/wiki/Cerebos" title="Cerebos">Cerebos</a> in 1903. By the mid-20th century, Cerebos was owned by the food conglomerate <a href="/wiki/Rank_Hovis_McDougall" title="Rank Hovis McDougall">Rank Hovis McDougall</a>, and the factory closed in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> During the 20th century the extraction of salt on the north bank of the River Tees by aqueous hydraulic means resulted in a number of underground salt cavities that are completely impervious to gas and liquids. Consequently, these cavities are now used to store both industrial gases and liquids by companies which are members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northeast_of_England_Process_Industry_Cluster" title="Northeast of England Process Industry Cluster">Northeast of England Process Industry Cluster</a> (NEPIC). Today the <a href="/wiki/Huntsman_Corporation" title="Huntsman Corporation">Huntsman</a> Tioxide is based close to Greatham, operating one of the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Chemical_plant" title="Chemical plant">chemical plants</a> for the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">titanium dioxide</a> which is the brilliant white <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> used in paints, Polo mints<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, cosmetics, UV sunscreens, plastics, golf balls and sports field line markings.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glass">Glass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Glass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Glass manufacture has been an important industry in the Northeast of England since stained glass glaziers worked on the Wearmouth and Jarrow monasteries in 674 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunderland,_Tyne_and_Wear" title="Sunderland, Tyne and Wear">Sunderland</a> and Tyneside were noted for glass-making between the 17th and 19th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In 1827 about two fifths of all English glass was made in the Tyneside area and in 1845 South Shields was making more plate glass than anywhere else in England. Sunderland was also rising to prominence as a glass-making centre, with James Hartley's <a href="/wiki/Hartley_Wood_and_Co" title="Hartley Wood and Co">Wear Glass Works</a> opening in 1836<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> , and by 1865 one third of the sheet glass in England was supplied by his Sunderland works.
The Candlish Glass Bottleworks was the largest in Europe, managed by <a href="/wiki/John_Candlish" title="John Candlish">John Candlish</a>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coal_mining">Coal mining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Coal mining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wynyard_Morris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1560" data-file-width="2207" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Wynyard_Morris.jpg/220px-Wynyard_Morris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Wynyard_Morris.jpg/330px-Wynyard_Morris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Wynyard_Morris.jpg/440px-Wynyard_Morris.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wynyard_Morris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wynyard Park circa 1880 now a fine Country House Hotel, Wynyard Hall</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">Coal mining</a> was one of the first industrial activities in Northeast England because the region was fortunate to have shallow seams of coal near the coast, which meant that material could be transported in and out by sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mining_in_Northeast_England_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mining_in_Northeast_England-38">[38]</a></sup> This led to the growth of ports in Sunderland, Newcastle, <a href="/wiki/Teesport" title="Teesport">Teesport</a> Middlesbrough, <a href="/wiki/Seaham" title="Seaham">Seaham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hartlepool" title="Hartlepool">Hartlepool</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blyth,_Northumberland" title="Blyth, Northumberland">Blyth</a>. The energy from coal underpinned the development of many of the industries around these ports. As discussed in the classic historical review of "Victorian Cities" by <a href="/wiki/Asa_Briggs" title="Asa Briggs">Asa Briggs</a>, Middlesbrough  was developed as a port downstream of Yarm and Stockton to take bigger coal ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The Northumberland-Durham coalfield was one of the earliest coal mining areas to be worked in the country, with the Romans extracting coal here which caused the area to become an important source of coal in the 13th and 14th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Many current towns and villages across the region were originally settlements set up for the coal miners. For example, Seaham is a port community that was developed to handle output of the coal mining interests of Charles William Vane-Tempest-Stewart the 3rd <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marquis_of_Londonderry" title="Marquis of Londonderry">Marquis of Londonderry</a>, a military leader and business man who became one of the UK's richest men due to his coal mining developments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The Marquis built his business interests using the inherited wealth of his wife Francis Anne. The Marquis also built one of the country's finest country houses in the region as a palace for his family and his royal connections. It is called <a href="/wiki/Wynyard_Park,_County_Durham" title="Wynyard Park, County Durham">Wynyard Hall</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> .
</p><p>London was one of the places which received coal from the area and there are references to shipments of coal being sent to the capital, for example 526 cauldrons of coal from Tyneside to London in 1376 for smiths involved in building Windsor Castle. Before the growth of mining companies the coal from the North East was often sent to London using monks. The coal was often called sea coal because it often washed up from undersea outcrops on the Northumbrian coast. This could explain the name Se-coles Lane in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mining_in_Northeast_England_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mining_in_Northeast_England-38">[38]</a></sup> It also led to the colloquial phrase "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taking_coals_to_Newcastle" title="Taking coals to Newcastle">taking coals to Newcastle</a>", meaning why take something to a place that already has an excess of it.
</p><p>Improvements in technology meant equipment could be built to go deeper than ever before. One example was the High Main seam at Walker Colliery on Tyneside, which became one of the deepest coal mines in the world, thanks to large engine cylinders which helped drain the mine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Other mining developments from this region include water level and ventilation techniques introduced by John Buddle who also helped to introduce the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miner%27s_safety_lamp" title="Miner's safety lamp">miner's safety lamp</a> which was invented here by <a href="/wiki/George_Stephenson" title="George Stephenson">Stephenson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Davy</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wearmouth_Colliery,_miners_in_cage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="629" data-file-width="633" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wearmouth_Colliery%2C_miners_in_cage.jpg/220px-Wearmouth_Colliery%2C_miners_in_cage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wearmouth_Colliery%2C_miners_in_cage.jpg/330px-Wearmouth_Colliery%2C_miners_in_cage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wearmouth_Colliery%2C_miners_in_cage.jpg/440px-Wearmouth_Colliery%2C_miners_in_cage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wearmouth_Colliery,_miners_in_cage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Miners in the cage ready for their descent, Monkwearmouth Colliery, 1993.</div></div></div>
<p>Sir Humphry Davy, after contemplating a communication he had received from Reverend Dr <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gray_(bishop_of_Bristol)" title="Robert Gray (bishop of Bristol)">Robert Gray</a> Rector of <a href="/wiki/Bishopwearmouth" title="Bishopwearmouth">Bishopwearmouth</a> (later Bishop of Bristol) regarding the problem of gas in mines, took up the challenge of solving the problem of providing light in "<a href="/wiki/Firedamp" title="Firedamp">fire-damp</a>" ridden collieries. He started the work with several days of discussions with John Buddle, the overseer at Wallsend Colliery, other colliery owners and finally the Reverend John Hodgson, Vicar of Jarrow. Davy also collected samples of "fire-damp" before returning to his laboratory in London. Two designs of his lamps emerged and were tested at the most hazardous pits in the country, then at Newcastle-upon- Tyne and <a href="/wiki/Whitehaven" title="Whitehaven">Whitehaven</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cumberland" title="Cumberland">Cumberland</a>, and were a resounding success. He later published his paper on "The safety lamp for coal mines and some researches on flame" in 1818, which made underground coal mines much more safe. George Stephenson a colliery engineer at Killingworth Main Colliery also invented a safety lamp which was successfully tested on 21 October 1815. This became known as the "Geordie" lamp. As a result, some in the Northeast then tried to challenge the delivery of some Ceremonial Plate to Davy but the Davy Lamper's won the day and on 25 September 1817 a dinner service as presented to Davy from the coal owners at the Queen's Head in Newcastle. Davy declined to take out a patent on his lamp design effectively giving it to the nation and the world's coal miners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The moment when the new safety lamp was first tested was recorded by John Buddle in a report from the Select Committee on Accidents in Mines on 4 September 1835 "I first tried the lamp in an explosive mixture on the surface; and then took it to the mine; it is impossible for me to express my feelings at the time when I first suspended the lamp in the mine and saw it red hot. I said to those around me: "We have at last subdued this monster [fire-damp]." With some describing it as one of the most significant moments in the industrialization of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>As an example of the many coal mines (colloquially known as pits) that were created in the Northeast's Monkwearmouth Colliery (or Wearmouth Colliery) was a large deep pit that went out under the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>. It was located on the north bank of the <a href="/wiki/River_Wear" title="River Wear">River Wear</a> and was the largest pit in Sunderland and one of the most important in <a href="/wiki/County_Durham" title="County Durham">County Durham</a>. The mine opened in 1835 and was the last to remain operating in the Durham Coalfield, with the last shift leaving the pit on 10 December 1993 and ending over 800 years of commercial underground coal mining in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The Colliery site has been cleared to make way for the Sunderland A.F.C's <a href="/wiki/Stadium_of_Light" title="Stadium of Light">Stadium of Light</a> which opened in July 1997. The mine is commemorated by a large sculpture of a miners lamp at the entrance to the stadium.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Durham_Coalfield" title="Durham Coalfield">Durham Coalfield</a> remains a national resource for the UK economy today and for the future. Most of the mines in the region were closed during the years of UK Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%931985)" title="UK miners' strike (1984–1985)">UK miners' strike (1984–1985)</a>) however several large open cast coal mines are still operational in the region.
</p><p>Today companies like Five-Quarter are investigating the use of the latest technology for underground <a href="/wiki/Coal_gasification" title="Coal gasification">coal gasification</a> to access the Durham Coalfield reserves. Professor Paul Younger of <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_University" title="Newcastle University">Newcastle University</a> in 2011 reported<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> that "Around 75 per cent of the coal in the North East is still underground, even though we have been mining it on an industrial scale longer than anyone else in the world. Previously a lot of this coal was too deep for conventional mining, or too far off shore. Even today this resource this could never be exploited by conventional means, but the technology to harness that resource has now become cost effective." Accessing these reserves is of particular importance to the local chemical industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iron_and_steel">Iron and steel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Iron and steel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Marley,_mining_engineer_(1823-1891).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="218" data-file-width="200" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/60/John_Marley%2C_mining_engineer_%281823-1891%29.jpg" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Marley,_mining_engineer_(1823-1891).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Marley, discoverer of the Cleveland Ironstone which led to the enormous growth of the iron industry in the North East of England</div></div></div>
<p>Before 1846 Walbottle, Elswick, Birtley, Ridsdale, Hareshaw, Wylam, Consett, Stanhope, Crookhall,Tow-Law and Witton Park all had iron works<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-This_is_Middlesbrough_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-This_is_Middlesbrough-50">[50]</a></sup> but the  discovery of a rich seam of iron ore to the south of the region gradually drew iron and steel manufacture towards Teesside.
</p><p>
In 1850 iron ore was discovered in the <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Hills" title="Cleveland Hills">Cleveland Hills</a> near <a href="/wiki/Eston" title="Eston">Eston</a> to the south of Middlesbrough and Iron gradually replaced coal as the lifeblood of Eston. The ore was discovered by geologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Marley_(geologist)" title="John Marley (geologist)">John Marley</a> and first utilised by John Vaughan, the principal <a href="/wiki/Ironmaster" title="Ironmaster">ironmaster</a> of Middlesbrough who along with his German business partner Henry Bolckow had already established a small iron foundry and rolling mill using iron stone from Durham and the Yorkshire coast, with the new discovery prompting them to build Teesside's first blast furnace in 1851.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-This_is_Middlesbrough_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-This_is_Middlesbrough-50">[50]</a></sup> Many more iron works followed, such as those built in the region by <a href="/wiki/Losh,_Wilson_and_Bell" title="Losh, Wilson and Bell">Losh, Wilson and Bell</a> (see Sir Issac <a href="/wiki/Lowthian_Bell" title="Lowthian Bell">Lowthian Bell</a>) who in 1853 were operating 5 furnaces in the region.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bell_Ironworks_at_Port_Clarence_Teesside_watercolour_by_John_Bell_(1814-1886).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="377" data-file-width="400" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Bell_Ironworks_at_Port_Clarence_Teesside_watercolour_by_John_Bell_%281814-1886%29.jpg/220px-Bell_Ironworks_at_Port_Clarence_Teesside_watercolour_by_John_Bell_%281814-1886%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Bell_Ironworks_at_Port_Clarence_Teesside_watercolour_by_John_Bell_%281814-1886%29.jpg/330px-Bell_Ironworks_at_Port_Clarence_Teesside_watercolour_by_John_Bell_%281814-1886%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/Bell_Ironworks_at_Port_Clarence_Teesside_watercolour_by_John_Bell_%281814-1886%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bell_Ironworks_at_Port_Clarence_Teesside_watercolour_by_John_Bell_(1814-1886).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Watercolour painting of the Bell Ironworks under construction at <a href="/wiki/Port_Clarence" title="Port Clarence">Port Clarence</a>, by John Bell, c. 1853</div></div></div>
<p>The success of John Vaughan and Henry Bolckow's first blast furnace meant that by 1873 Middlesbrough was producing 2 million tonnes of pig iron a year. Iron was in big demand in Britain in the late 19th century, particularly for the rapid expansion of the railways. More and more blast furnaces were opened in the vicinity of Middlesbrough to meet this demand such that by the end of the century Teesside was producing about a third of the nation's iron output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-51">[51]</a></sup> Middlesbrough, which became known by its nickname "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ironopolis" title="Ironopolis">Ironopolis</a>", was visited in 1862 by then prime minister <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a> who said "This remarkable place, the youngest child of England's enterprise, is an infant, but if an infant, an infant Hercules"
By the 1870s steel, a much stronger and more resilient metal, was in big demand and Middlesbrough had to compete with Sheffield as the major producer. In 1875 Bolckow and Vaughan opened the first Bessemer Steel plant in Middlesbrough and the River Tees then become known as  "The Steel River"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> leaving its old nickname "Ironopolis" behind. In 1881 <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Reid_(Liberal_politician)" title="Hugh Reid (Liberal politician)">Hugh Reid (Liberal politician)</a> described how "The iron of Eston has diffused itself all over the world. it furnishes the railways of the world; it runs by neapolitan and papal dungeons; it startles the bandit in his haunt in cicilia; it crosses over the plains of Africa; it stretches over the plains of India. it has crept out of the Cleveland Hills where it has slept since Roman days, and now like a strong and invincible serpent, coils itself around the world"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1929 the great depression began to effect Britain and the famous name of Bolckow-Vaughan merged with neighbour Dorman-Long &amp; Co. who then became Britain's biggest iron and steel maker and employed 33,000 people. In 1954 the post-war boom saw Dorman-Long build a state of the art steelworks at Lackenby and then new blast furnaces at Clay Lane. 1967 saw Dorman-Long become part of the nationalized British Steel Corporation as production boomed in Britain and in 1979 the largest blast furnace in Europe was erected at BSC's new Redcar plant. This plant which was subsequently acquired and operated by Chorus, Tata Steel and then Sahaviriya Steel Industries (SSI) who are still the plant operating today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Steel_Industrial_Archive">British Steel Industrial Archive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: British Steel Industrial Archive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The British Steel Collection ,now housed at <a href="/wiki/Teesside_University" title="Teesside University">Teesside University</a>, contains the records of over forty iron and steel companies based in the Teesside area of the North East of England and covers the period c. 1840–1970. The history of Teesside and its rapid growth during the 19th century is directly linked to the expansion of the railways from Darlington and Stockton towards the mouth of the Tees estuary and the subsequent discovery of ironstone in the Cleveland Hills which attracted iron companies to the area. The British Steel Collection archives the company records of iron and steel companies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolckow_Vaughan" title="Bolckow Vaughan">Bolckow &amp; Vaughan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bell_Brothers" title="Bell Brothers">Bell Brothers</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cochrane_%26_Co._Ltd.&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cochrane &amp; Co. Ltd. (page does not exist)">Cochrane &amp; Co. Ltd.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorman_Long" title="Dorman Long">Dorman, Long &amp; Co. Ltd.</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=South_Durham_Steel_%26_Iron_Co._Ltd.&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="South Durham Steel &amp; Iron Co. Ltd. (page does not exist)">South Durham Steel &amp; Iron Co. Ltd.</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cargo_Fleet_Iron_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cargo Fleet Iron Company (page does not exist)">Cargo Fleet Iron Company</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skinningrove_Steelworks" title="Skinningrove Steelworks">Skinningrove Iron Co. Ltd.</a> With records of associated institutions such as the Middlesbrough Exchange Co. Ltd. and the Cleveland Mineowners' Association also being preserved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipbuilding">Shipbuilding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Shipbuilding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">Shipbuilding</a> was once one of this region's largest industrial sectors. Ships were built on the <a href="/wiki/River_Wear" title="River Wear">River Wear</a> at Sunderland from at least 1346 and on the <a href="/wiki/River_Tees" title="River Tees">River Tees</a> at Stockton from at least 1470,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shipbuilding_1790_to_1899_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shipbuilding_1790_to_1899-56">[56]</a></sup>  with the Northeast of England more generally being the birthplace of some of the world's greatest vessels. This was recognised in 2013 with the regions shipbuilding heritage and global impact being recognized by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> and placed on their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memory_of_the_World_Register" title="Memory of the World Register">Memory of the World Register</a>, ranking the regions shipbuilding heritage alongside iconic items such as the Domesday Book in terms of historical importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wood_to_iron_and_steel">Wood to iron and steel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Wood to iron and steel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The early ships were built of wood but in the 19th century there was a move towards building ships of iron then steel.  Ships were built across the region, especially along <a href="/wiki/Tyneside" title="Tyneside">Tyneside</a> in Jarrow and <a href="/wiki/Wearside" title="Wearside">Wearside</a> in Sunderland and also in smaller ports like Blyth, <a href="/wiki/Whitby" title="Whitby">Whitby</a> and Hartlepool. Sunderland's early development was due to coal but it later transitioned to become the largest shipbuilding town in the world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> giving the town its fame. The first recorded shipbuilder was Thomas Menville at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hendon,_Tyne_and_Wear" title="Hendon, Tyne and Wear">Hendon</a> in 1346.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> By 1790 Sunderland was building around nineteen ships per year making it the most important shipbuilding centre in the United Kingdom. By 1840 there were 65 shipyards such that over 150 wooden vessels were built at Sunderland in 1850. At this time 2,025 shipwrights worked in the town and some 2,000 others were employed in related industries. Sunderland's first iron ships were built from 1852 and wooden shipbuilding ceased here in 1876. Sunderland shipbuilders included Austin and Son, William Pickersgill  and William Doxford.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shipbuilding_1790_to_1899_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shipbuilding_1790_to_1899-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was between 1790 and 1805 that Thomas Haw of Stockton began building ships for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_wars" title="Napoleonic wars">Napoleonic wars</a>. Shipbuilding did not begin in Middlesbrough until 1833 when a wooden sailing ship called <i>The Middlesbro'</i> was built. Teesside's first iron ship was built in Thorneby in 1854, it was a screw steamer called <i>The Advance</i>, and Teesside's first steel ship was <i>Little Lucy</i> built in 1858. One famous Teesside-built ship was the 377-foot (115 m) long <i>Talpore</i>, built by Pearse and Co of Stockton in 1860. It was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troop_ship" title="Troop ship">troop ship</a> for the River Indus, and was the world's largest river steamer at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> An archive of the ships built on Teesside has been created,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In Hartlepool Thomas Richardson of Castle Eden and John Parkin of Sunderland established a shipyard at Old Hartlepool in 1835 and built The Castle Eden ship. The shipbuilding company of William Gray was established here in 1862 and Gray became one of the most influential men in the town. He was the first mayor of West Hartlepool in 1887. William Gray shipbuilders won the Blue Ribband prize for maximum output in 1878, 1882, 1888, 1895, 1898 and 1900. The yard closed in 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mauritania_(ship)_(between_1906_and_1914).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="375" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Mauritania_%28ship%29_%28between_1906_and_1914%29.jpg/220px-Mauritania_%28ship%29_%28between_1906_and_1914%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Mauritania_%28ship%29_%28between_1906_and_1914%29.jpg/330px-Mauritania_%28ship%29_%28between_1906_and_1914%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/12/Mauritania_%28ship%29_%28between_1906_and_1914%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mauritania_(ship)_(between_1906_and_1914).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>RMS <i>Mauretania</i> on its Tyneside builder's ways before launch in 1906</div></div></div>
<p>On Tyneside, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Mark_Palmer" title="Charles Mark Palmer">Charles Mark Palmer</a>, born in <a href="/wiki/South_Shields" title="South Shields">South Shields</a>, established a yard at Jarrow in 1851 and built its first iron collier, <i>The John Bowes</i>, in the following year. It was the first ever seagoing screw collier and was built for John Bowes of Barnard Castle for shipping coal to London. Palmer was also famed for building the first rolled armour plates for <a href="/wiki/Warship" title="Warship">warships</a> in 1854. William Smith and Co launched the 1,600-ton <i>Blenheim</i> in 1848. W. G. Armstrong, the famous northern engineer, acquired an interest in the Tyneside shipbuilding firm of Mitchells in 1882, and the company of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W.G._Armstrong" title="W.G. Armstrong">W. G. Armstrong</a>, Mitchell and Co was formed. The yard built <a href="/wiki/Battleship" title="Battleship">battleships</a> as well as a ship called <i>Gluckauf</i>, which was arguably the world's first oil tanker. It was <a href="/wiki/Ceremonial_ship_launching" title="Ceremonial ship launching">launched</a> by the yard in 1886. Scotsman Charles Mitchell started building ships at Walker-on-Tyne in 1852 and purchased a 6.5-acre (2.6 ha) site at <a href="/wiki/Wallsend" title="Wallsend">Wallsend</a> in 1873 to soak up excess orders from his Walker shipyard. The new yard failed financially and was handed to his brother-in-law Charles Swan. Charles and his brother Henry were directors of the Wallsend Slipway Company, a repair yard established by Mitchell in 1871. In 1878 Charles arranged a partnership with Sunderland shipbuilder George Hunter, but in 1879 Charles died after falling overboard from a channel steamer whilst returning from the Continent with his wife. Hunter went into temporary partnership with Swan's wife before becoming Managing Director in 1880. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swan_Hunter_Shipyard" title="Swan Hunter Shipyard">Swan Hunters</a> built their first steel ship at Wallsend in 1884 and their first oil tanker in 1889.
</p><p>Most early ships built at the Swan Hunter yard were smaller, like colliers and barges, but in 1898 it built its first <a href="/wiki/Ocean_liner" title="Ocean liner">ocean liner</a>, <i>Ultonia,</i> with a further 21 liners being built between 1898–1903. The most famous ship ever launched there was undoubtedly <a href="/wiki/RMS_Mauretania_(1906)" title="RMS Mauretania (1906)"><i>Mauretania</i></a>, a transatlantic ocean liner that launched on 20 September 1906. The ship was 790-foot (240 m) long, with a <a href="/wiki/Beam_(nautical)" title="Beam (nautical)">Beam (nautical)</a> of 88 ft (27 m) and a <a href="/wiki/Gross_register_tonnage" title="Gross register tonnage">gross register tonnage</a> of 31,938 tons. It carried 2,000 passengers on its <a href="/wiki/Maiden_voyage" title="Maiden voyage">maiden voyage</a> on 16 November 1907 and won the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Riband" title="Blue Riband">Blue Riband</a> for the fastest crossing of the Atlantic, a record held for 22 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shipbuilding_1790_to_1899_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Shipbuilding_1790_to_1899-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Maritime_innovation">Maritime innovation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Maritime innovation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A major pioneering innovation in marine engineering was the steam turbine, invented by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Algernon_Parsons" title="Charles Algernon Parsons">Charles Algernon Parsons</a>. He patented the first steam turbine on Tyneside in 1884. Parsons, born in Ireland in 1854, was the youngest son of the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Rosse" title="Earl of Rosse">Earl of Rosse</a> and a keen inventor, who was a junior partner in the Tyneside engineering firm of Clarke Chapman. In 1894 Parsons' Marine Turbine Company launched <a href="/wiki/Turbinia" title="Turbinia"><i>Turbinia</i></a>, the first ship to be powered by electric turbines. She can be still be seen (and boarded) at the Discovery Museum in Newcastle upon Tyne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="20th-century_decline">20th-century decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 20th-century decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Shipyard closures in the 20th century took place during economic slumps and occurred in two phases, between 1909–1933 and 1960–1993. Early closures included Smiths Dock at <a href="/wiki/North_Shields" title="North Shields">North Shields</a> in 1909, which became a ship repair yard, Armstrongs of Elswick in 1921, Richardson Duck of Stockton (1925), Priestman's of Sunderland (1933) and Palmers of Jarrow and <a href="/wiki/Hebburn" title="Hebburn">Hebburn</a> (1933). There were 28 North East closures in this period of which 14 were on the Tyne, 7 on the Wear, 6 on the Tees and 1 at Hartlepool. Six shipyards closed in the 1960s including W.Gray of Hartlepool (1961), Short Brothers of Sunderland (1964) and The Blyth Shipbuiding Company (1966). There were five closures in the region in the 1970s including the Furness yard at Haverton Hill, near Stockton, in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shipbuilding_1790_to_1899_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-Shipbuilding_1790_to_1899-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="James_Cook">James Cook</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: James Cook">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Captainjamescookportrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1020" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Captainjamescookportrait.jpg/220px-Captainjamescookportrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Captainjamescookportrait.jpg/330px-Captainjamescookportrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Captainjamescookportrait.jpg/440px-Captainjamescookportrait.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Captainjamescookportrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>James Cook, portrait by Nathaniel Dance-Holland circa 1775, National Maritime Museum, Greenwich.</div></div></div>
<p>The region was home to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_James_Cook" title="Captain James Cook">Captain James Cook</a>, who sailed his ship (<a href="/wiki/HMS_Endeavour" title="HMS Endeavour">HMS <i>Endeavour</i></a>) from <a href="/wiki/Whitby" title="Whitby">Whitby</a> to discover and name for the western world the <a href="/wiki/Antipodes" title="Antipodes">antipodean</a> continents and islands as well as many islands in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_engineering">Science and engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Science and engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The coal and shipbuilding industry that once dominated the North East suffered a marked decline during the second half of the 20th century. <a href="/wiki/Tyneside" title="Tyneside">Tyneside</a> is now re-inventing itself as an international centre of art, culture and through <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Life" title="Centre for Life">The Centre For Life</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a> <a href="/wiki/Research" title="Research">research</a>, especially in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Healthcare" title="Healthcare">healthcare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a>. <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_University" title="Newcastle University">Newcastle University</a> is now a leading institution in the development of <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cell</a> technology being the first in the United Kingdom and the second institution in Europe to obtain a licence to do such work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> As with the rest of the region, Sunderland suffered economic decline during the last century, but is now becoming an important area for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaternary_industry" title="Quaternary industry">quaternary industry</a>, bioscience, computing and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_technology" title="High technology">high</a> tech industries. The economy of Sunderland is now dominated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nissan_Sunderland" title="Nissan Sunderland">Nissan</a>'s European car manufacturing facility and supply chain which is also leading that company's development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_vehicles" title="Electric vehicles">electric vehicles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The economy of Teesside continues to be largely based on the <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commodity_chemicals" title="Commodity chemicals">commodity chemical</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steel_industry" title="Steel industry">steel industries</a> that form a significant part of the Northeast of England Process Industry Cluster (NEPIC), but newer industries such as offshore engineering and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_computing" title="Digital computing">digital computing</a>, particularly in the field of <a href="/wiki/Game_design" title="Game design">Game design</a>, have emerged from Teesside University.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>, although largely rural and an important tourist location with its castles, beaches, and history has a nationally significant concentration of pharmaceutical manufacturing companies around Cramlington and Prudhoe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The county has also seen a huge investment into the creation of the UKs largest reservoir, <a href="/wiki/Kielder_Water" title="Kielder Water">Kielder Water</a>, enabling the Northeast to attract more industry. Kielder forest around the reservoir is known to have the darkest night sky in England,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> making it an ideal location for both professional and amateur astronomers. The <a href="/wiki/Durham,_England" title="Durham, England">City of Durham</a> with its highly regarded <a href="/wiki/Durham_University" title="Durham University">University</a>, Castle and Cathedral attracts many tourists and also a significant number of knowledge intensive businesses (KIBS) in architecture, engineering, technology and measurement science. At <a href="/wiki/Sedgefield" title="Sedgefield">Sedgefield</a> in County Durham, <a href="/wiki/Netpark" title="Netpark">Netpark</a> is home to the Centre for Process Innovation's <a href="/wiki/Printable_Electronics_Technology_Centre" title="Printable Electronics Technology Centre">Printable Electronics Technology Centre</a>, a nationally important centre for the development of <a href="/wiki/Printed_electronics" title="Printed electronics">printed electronics</a> and a number of other emerging electronics companies such as Kromek.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Middlesbrough_Transporter_Bridge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Middlesbrough_Transporter_Bridge.jpg/220px-Middlesbrough_Transporter_Bridge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Middlesbrough_Transporter_Bridge.jpg/330px-Middlesbrough_Transporter_Bridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Middlesbrough_Transporter_Bridge.jpg/440px-Middlesbrough_Transporter_Bridge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Middlesbrough_Transporter_Bridge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middlesbrough_Transporter_Bridge" title="Middlesbrough Transporter Bridge">Middlesbrough Transporter Bridge</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyne_Bridges_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Tyne_Bridges_01.jpg/300px-Tyne_Bridges_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Tyne_Bridges_01.jpg/450px-Tyne_Bridges_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Tyne_Bridges_01.jpg/600px-Tyne_Bridges_01.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyne_Bridges_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tyne Bridge built by Middlesbrough Company Dorman Long</div></div></div>
<p>Today, the members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northeast_of_England_Process_Industry_Cluster" title="Northeast of England Process Industry Cluster">Northeast of England Process Industry Cluster</a> (NEPIC) represent about one third of the regional industrial economy. The NEPIC is constituted by commodity chemical, <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemical</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speciality_chemical" title="Speciality chemical">speciality chemical</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceutical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymer</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renewable_material" title="Renewable material">renewable material</a> and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> companies and the associated supply chains. The Teesside industry is located on three large chemical sites at <a href="/wiki/Wilton,_Redcar_and_Cleveland" title="Wilton, Redcar and Cleveland">Wilton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billingham" title="Billingham">Billingham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seal_Sands" title="Seal Sands">Seal Sands</a> at the mouth of the River Tees and <a href="/wiki/Teesport" title="Teesport">Teesport</a>, the third largest port in the UK and the tenth in Western Europe and is important logistical infrastructure supporting the commodity chemical and steel members of NEPIC. In the 21st century <a href="/wiki/PD_Ports" title="PD Ports">PD Ports</a>, owners of Teesport, have been developing it as a Port Centric Logistical Centre. This strategy has seen a number of significant importing and distribution facilities for the north of the UK being built here, including distribution centres for the distribution operations of Asda/Walmart and Tesco supermarket chains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>NEPIC has two offices in the region: one in the north in Sunderland, serving the pharmaceutical and speciality chemical industries on Tyneside and in south Northumberland, and one in the south at <a href="/wiki/Wilton_International" title="Wilton International">Wilton</a> near Redcar, serving the commodity chemical and steel industry of Teesside and operating amongst several process sector and supply chain companies that work out of the process industry research centre, The Wilton Centre, one of Europe's largest technical development laboratory facilities. The head office of the <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Process_Innovation" title="Centre for Process Innovation">Centre for Process Innovation</a>, part of the UK's <a href="/wiki/High_Value_Manufacturing_Catapult" title="High Value Manufacturing Catapult">High Value Manufacturing Catapult</a>, is based in this multi-occupancy technical development centre along with their pioneering National Industrial Biotechnology Facility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Innovations">Innovations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Innovations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This region has a strong history in technological innovation:
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Match" title="Match">friction match</a> was invented in Stockton-on-Tees in 1826 by <a href="/wiki/John_Walker_(inventor)" title="John Walker (inventor)">John Walker</a>.
