<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Athletics">Athletics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Athletics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athletics_(sport)" title="Athletics (sport)">Athletics</a> is a sport of rising popularity since the olympic and international success of Northeast athletes <a href="/wiki/Brendan_Foster" title="Brendan Foster">Brendan Foster</a> in the 1970s and <a href="/wiki/Steve_Cram" title="Steve Cram">Steve Cram</a> in the 1980s. Both won international medals and broke world records in middle and long distance running. Brendan Foster established the annual <a href="/wiki/Great_North_Run" title="Great North Run">Great North Run</a>, one of the best known half marathons in which thousands of participants run from Newcastle to South Shields. Brendan Foster, a former school teacher in this region, is also recognised as the driving force behind the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Gateshead_International_Stadium" title="Gateshead International Stadium">Gateshead International Athletics Stadium</a> which now regularly hosts International Athletics meets and other sporting events.
</p><p>In 2013 the 33rd <a href="/wiki/Great_North_Run" title="Great North Run">Great North Run</a> had 56,000 participants most of whom were raising money for charity. The elite races had Olympic gold medalists and world champion long distance runners participateing including in the men's race, <a href="/wiki/Mo_Farah" title="Mo Farah">Mo Farah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenenisa_Bekele" title="Kenenisa Bekele">Kenenisa Bekele</a> and a regular supporter of the event <a href="/wiki/Haile_Gebrselassie" title="Haile Gebrselassie">Haile Gebrselassie</a>. Ethiopian Bekele won the men's event just ahead of Farah. Kenya's <a href="/wiki/Priscah_Jeptoo" title="Priscah Jeptoo">Priscah Jeptoo</a> came first the women's race and multi Olympic gold medalist <a href="/wiki/David_Weir_(athlete)" title="David Weir (athlete)">David Weir</a> won the wheelchair event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> The founder of the event, <a href="/wiki/Brendan_Foster" title="Brendan Foster">Brendan Foster</a> is reported to be looking forward to 2014 when the Great North Run have its millionth finisher – becoming the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAAF" title="IAAF">International Athletics Association</a> Event (IAAF) event in the world to reach such a milestone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
The 2014 Great North Run did indeed make history with olympic gold medal and UK athletics star Mo Farah winning the men's race in a new personal best of just 1 hour. He was challenged all the way but he held off strong competition from Kenya's Mike Kigen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The millionth finisher of the race was a mum-of-two and grandma-of-two Tracey Cramond of Darlington. 51 year old Tracey ran the race to raise funds for the local Teesside charity Butterwick Hospice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_government">Local government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Local government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The official <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_England" title="Regions of England">region</a> consists of the following subdivisions:
</p>

</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_political_history">Recent political history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Recent political history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Durham_Cathedral_from_Durham_School.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Durham_Cathedral_from_Durham_School.jpg/220px-Durham_Cathedral_from_Durham_School.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Durham_Cathedral_from_Durham_School.jpg/330px-Durham_Cathedral_from_Durham_School.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Durham_Cathedral_from_Durham_School.jpg/440px-Durham_Cathedral_from_Durham_School.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Durham_Cathedral_from_Durham_School.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Durham Cathedral</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Track_in_Kielder_Forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_204470.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Track_in_Kielder_Forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_204470.jpg/220px-Track_in_Kielder_Forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_204470.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Track_in_Kielder_Forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_204470.jpg/330px-Track_in_Kielder_Forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_204470.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Track_in_Kielder_Forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_204470.jpg/440px-Track_in_Kielder_Forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_204470.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Track_in_Kielder_Forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_204470.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kielder_Forest" title="Kielder Forest">Kielder Forest</a> – the largest man-made forest in Europe</div></div></div>
<p>The region was created in 1994 and was originally defined as the non-metropolitan counties of <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Durham" title="County Durham">County Durham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cleveland,_England" title="Cleveland, England">Cleveland</a>, along with the metropolitan county of <a href="/wiki/Tyne_and_Wear" title="Tyne and Wear">Tyne and Wear</a>. A reform of local government abolished Cleveland and created several unitary districts, as detailed in the above table.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2004_regional_assembly_referendum">2004 regional assembly referendum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: 2004 regional assembly referendum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In November 2004 a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_England_devolution_referendums,_2004" title="Northern England devolution referendums, 2004">referendum</a> on whether a directly elected regional assembly should be set up for North East England resulted in a decisive "no" vote. The number of people who voted against the plans was 696,519 (78%), while 197,310 (22%) voted in favour. John Prescott, the Deputy Prime Minister at the time, admitted that his plans for regional devolution had suffered an "emphatic defeat". Bernard Jenkin, the Conservative spokesman for the regions, said the vote would mean the end of plans for a North East Assembly. He told the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>: "The whole idea of regional government has been blown out of the water by this vote".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combined_authorities">Combined authorities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Combined authorities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/North_East_Combined_Authority" title="North East Combined Authority">North East Combined Authority</a> was established in 2014 and covers much of the region, except for the <a href="/wiki/Tees_Valley" title="Tees Valley">Tees Valley</a> boroughs of Darlington, Hartlepool, Stockton-on-Tees, Redcar &amp; Cleveland, and Middlesbrough. These instead established the <a href="/wiki/Tees_Valley_Combined_Authority" title="Tees Valley Combined Authority">Tees Valley Combined Authority</a> in 2016.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The region has a diverse landscape that includes maritime cliffs and extensive <a href="/wiki/Moorland" title="Moorland">moorland</a> that contains a number of <a href="/wiki/Rare_species" title="Rare species">rare species</a> of <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">flora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a>. Of particular importance are the saltmarshes of <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">Lindisfarne</a>, the <a href="/wiki/River_Tees" title="River Tees">Tees Estuary</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heath_(habitat)" title="Heath (habitat)">heaths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bog" title="Bog">bogs</a> and traditional upland hay meadows of the North Pennines, and the Arctic-alpine flora of Upper Teesdale.
</p><p>The beauty of the <a href="/wiki/Northumberland_Coast" title="Northumberland Coast">Northumbrian coastline</a> has led to its designation as an <a href="/wiki/Area_of_Outstanding_Natural_Beauty" title="Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty">area of outstanding natural beauty</a> (AONB)
stretching 100 miles from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berwick-Upon-Tweed" title="Berwick-Upon-Tweed">Berwick-Upon-Tweed</a> to the <a href="/wiki/River_Coquet" title="River Coquet">River Coquet</a> estuary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Among the 290 bird species identified on the <a href="/wiki/Farne_Islands" title="Farne Islands">Farne Islands</a>, is the rare <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabird</a> the <a href="/wiki/Roseate_tern" title="Roseate tern">roseate tern</a>. One of the foremost bird sanctuaries and observatory for migratory and wading birds in the UK is now operated at "Saltholme" which is part of a wider site of special scientific interest called <a href="/wiki/Seal_Sands" title="Seal Sands">Seal Sands</a>. The <a class="external text" href="http://www.rspb.org.uk/reserves/guide/s/saltholme/" rel="nofollow">Saltholme reserve</a> is managed by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Protection_of_Birds" title="Royal Society for the Protection of Birds">Royal Society for the Protection of Birds</a>(RSPB). This project has been pronounced as one of the best places to view birds by <a href="/wiki/Bill_Oddie" title="Bill Oddie">Bill Oddie</a>, the celebrity bird watcher and former host of the BBC's <i>Spring Watch</i> Programme. In December 2012 he also presented the project with a prize as the UK's favorite <a href="/wiki/National_Lottery_(United_Kingdom)" title="National Lottery (United Kingdom)">National Lottery</a> funded project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>  The seal colony at Seal Sands on the mouth of the River Tees is thriving and stands at more than 60 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harbour_seals" title="Harbour seals">harbour seals</a> and this is the only breeding colony of this species on the northeast coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>"<a href="/wiki/Rainton_Meadows" title="Rainton Meadows">Rainton Meadows</a>" is also a recently created bird-watching site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The region is also the English stronghold of black grouse<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> and contains 80–90% of the UK population of yellow marsh <a href="/wiki/Saxifraga" title="Saxifraga">saxifrage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Magnesian_Limestone" title="Magnesian Limestone">Magnesian Limestone</a> grasslands of East Durham are a unique habitat not found nowhere else in the world which is particularly important to many species of butterfly and moths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Northeast of England also features woodland such as <a href="/wiki/Kielder_Forest" title="Kielder Forest">Kielder Forest</a>, the largest man-made forest in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> This is located within Northumberland National Park and contains an important habitat for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endangered" title="Endangered">endangered</a> <a href="/wiki/Red_squirrel" title="Red squirrel">red squirrel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The North East and with the <a href="/wiki/South_West_England" title="South West England">South West</a> are the English regions to have seen the least immigration from outside Great Britain and Ireland for over 50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The Northeast of England as a region has the lowest rate of HIV infection in the UK,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> but has the highest rate of heart attacks among men and of lung cancer among women in England, along with the highest male lung cancer rate in the UK .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>  As of November 2017 the region has the joint highest unemployment rate in the UK at 5.5%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> and as of April 2013 youth unemployment in the North East is 24.8% of the unemployed, with 51,000 out of work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> In 2010 the region had the second highest trade union membership among UK men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Higher education students from the North East are most likely to pick a university in their home region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> The Northeast, as part of the "North" demographic region, has the highest proportion of Christians in Great Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teenage_pregnancy">Teenage pregnancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Teenage pregnancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Office for National Statistics in April 2013 report that the estimated number of conceptions to women aged under 18 in England and Wales in 2011 is the lowest since records began in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Office_for_National_Statistics_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Office_for_National_Statistics-149">[149]</a></sup>  Conception statistics include pregnancies that result in either one or more live births or stillbirths or a legal abortion.
</p><p>A comparison of rates across regions in England shows that the North East had the highest of under 18 conception rates in 2011, with 38.4 per thousand women aged 15–17. The South East had the lowest rate for women aged under 18 in 2011 with 26.1 per thousand women aged 15–17.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Office_for_National_Statistics_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Office_for_National_Statistics-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_deprivation">Social deprivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Social deprivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A study into social deprivation was published in 2010 to help the local partners developing a Regional Strategy for the North East better understand the factors influencing deprivation in the region. The study had two main aspects: Firstly to establish if there are different types of deprived neighbourhoods in the Northeast, and if so, how deprived neighbourhoods can be better recognised. Secondly to present a summary of ‘what works' in tackling deprivation in each of these types of area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>The report discusses the factors influencing deprivation and points out that it is a significant problem for the North East with 34% of the regions Lower Super Output Areas (LSOAs) are amongst England's 20% most deprived in the 2007 <a href="/wiki/Indices_of_deprivation_2007" title="Indices of deprivation 2007">Indices of Deprivation</a>(these indices have been <a href="/wiki/Indices_of_deprivation_2010" title="Indices of deprivation 2010">updated in 2010</a>). It takes many years for areas to become deprived, suggesting that the underlying causes of area-based deprivation are long-term such as:
</p>
<ul><li>Major changes in the employment base, which has changed the nature and spatial distribution of jobs in the UK and within specific regions and localities.</li>
<li>The ‘residential sorting' effects of the public and private housing markets.</li></ul>
<p>Industrial restructuring has disproportionately affected some communities and groups. In particular:
</p>
<ul><li>Job losses in manufacturing and coalmining were most severely felt in the north of England, Scotland and Wales – and particular communities within these areas.</li>
<li>As a result of the types of jobs that were lost, some demographic groups – particularly older working age males in skilled manual work – were more likely to be affected than others.</li></ul>
<p>The region's most deprived council districts, as measured by the LSOA data<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> before County Durham and Northumberland became unitary authorities in 2007, are in descending order <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Easington_(district)" title="Easington (district)">Easington</a> (7th in England), Middlesbrough (9th), Hartlepool (23rd), <a href="/wiki/Wear_Valley" title="Wear Valley">Wear Valley</a> (33rd), Sunderland (35th), Newcastle upon Tyne (37th), South Tyneside (38th), Wansbeck (46th), Redcar and Cleveland (50th), Gateshead (52nd), <a href="/wiki/Sedgefield_(borough)" title="Sedgefield (borough)">Sedgefield</a> (54th), <a href="/wiki/Derwentside" title="Derwentside">Derwentside</a> (73rd), <a href="/wiki/Blyth_Valley" title="Blyth Valley">Blyth Valley</a> (80th), and Stockton on Tees (98th).
</p><p>The least deprived council districts in 2007 were, in descending order, Tynedale, <a href="/wiki/Castle_Morpeth" title="Castle Morpeth">Castle Morpeth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teesdale_(district)" title="Teesdale (district)">Teesdale</a>, then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alnwick_(district)" title="Alnwick (district)">Alnwick</a>. Since the April 2009 abolition of these four districts, Northumberland is the least deprived, followed by North Tyneside.
</p><p>Unemployment is a severe problem in the North East, where many children grow up in households where no adult works. in 2010 Easington had the highest rate in the country, as 40.3% of its households with children had no working adult, followed by Sedgefield with 34%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> In 2013 the Office for National Statistics report issued the statements highlighted below<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Employment rate highest in the South East (74.8%) and lowest in the North East (66.6%).</li>
<li>Unemployment rate highest in the North East (10.1%) and lowest in the South West (6.2%).</li>
<li>Inactivity rate highest in the North East (25.8%) and lowest in the South East (19.8%).</li>
<li>Claimant Count rate highest in the North East (7.2%) and lowest in the South East (2.7%).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_genetics">Population genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Population genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>North East England, together with Tweeddale, was the ancient British tribal kingdom of Bernicia (Bryneich) and is notable for providing the stable ancestry of its present indigenous population, which has been identified by DNA analysis to be an offshoot of the group  "Scotland, Cumbria and the North of Ireland", but not so closely related to the other peoples of the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elections">Elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The North East has a strong tendency to vote <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015 election</a>, 47% of the electorate voted Labour, while 25% voted <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a>, 17% UKIP, 6% <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrat</a> and 4% Green. At the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2009_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2009 (United Kingdom)">2009 European election</a>, Labour got 25% of the region's vote, the Conservatives 20%, the Liberal Democrats 18%, and <a href="/wiki/UK_Independence_Party" title="UK Independence Party">UK Independence Party</a> 15%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="Constituencies_in_North_East_England_(29)" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th class="navbox-title" colspan="2" scope="col"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Constituencies_in_North_East_England" title="Template:Constituencies in North East England"><abbr style=";;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;" title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Constituencies_in_North_East_England" title="Template talk:Constituencies in North East England"><abbr style=";;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;" title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Constituencies_in_North_East_England&amp;action=edit"><abbr style=";;background:none transparent;border:none;-moz-box-shadow:none;-webkit-box-shadow:none;box-shadow:none; padding:0;" title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div><div id="Constituencies_in_North_East_England_(29)" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em">Constituencies in North East England (29)</div></th></tr><tr><th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%"><a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> (26)</th><td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eurostat_NUTS">Eurostat NUTS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Eurostat NUTS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Angel_of_the_North_silhouette.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2217" data-file-width="3709" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Angel_of_the_North_silhouette.jpg/220px-Angel_of_the_North_silhouette.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Angel_of_the_North_silhouette.jpg/330px-Angel_of_the_North_silhouette.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Angel_of_the_North_silhouette.jpg/440px-Angel_of_the_North_silhouette.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Angel_of_the_North_silhouette.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Angel_of_the_North" title="Angel of the North">Angel of the North</a></i></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Tyne_and_Wear" title="Transport in Tyne and Wear">Transport in Tyne and Wear</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail">Rail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Rail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_Main_Line" title="East Coast Main Line">East Coast Main Line</a> (ECML) calls at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newcastle_Central_Railway_Station" title="Newcastle Central Railway Station">Newcastle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Durham_railway_station" title="Durham railway station">Durham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Darlington_railway_station" title="Darlington railway station">Darlington</a>, and provides fast connections to London and Edinburgh, however, in May 2018 the UK Government announced that the line was to be re-nationalized for the second time since privatisation in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Durham_Coast_Line" title="Durham Coast Line">Durham Coast Line</a> connects Sunderland, Hartlepool and Middlesbrough with the main line. <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>-based <a href="/wiki/London_North_Eastern_Railway" title="London North Eastern Railway">London North Eastern Railway</a> serves the full length of the ECML and operates most of the stations on the route. <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_(train_operating_company)" title="Grand Central (train operating company)">Grand Central</a> has linked Sunderland, and Teesside with London since December 2007, and is non-stop from York onwards. It does not have electric trains, and uses the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northallerton%E2%80%93Eaglescliffe_Line" title="Northallerton–Eaglescliffe Line">Northallerton–Eaglescliffe Line</a> and Durham Coast Line. Local services along these and most other local routes in the North East are provided by <a href="/wiki/Northern_(train_operating_company)" title="Northern (train operating company)">Northern</a>, based in Manchester. <a href="/wiki/TransPennine_Express" title="TransPennine Express">TransPennine Express</a>, also based in Manchester, have long-distance services from Newcastle, Scarborough and Middlesbrough to Manchester, via <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire" title="West Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a>. 
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tyne_and_Wear_Metro" title="Tyne and Wear Metro">Tyne and Wear Metro</a> is a light rail network which serves the metropolitan county of Tyne and Wear. It has stations in Newcastle and Sunderland city centres and other towns and suburbs in the county, as well as at <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_Airport" title="Newcastle Airport">Newcastle Airport</a> and attractions such as the <a href="/wiki/St_James%27_Park" title="St James' Park">St James' Park</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Stadium_of_Light" title="Stadium of Light">Stadium of Light</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gateshead_International_Stadium" title="Gateshead International Stadium">Gateshead International Stadium</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Road">Road</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Road">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The North East's main arterial carriageway is the <a href="/wiki/A1_road_(Great_Britain)" title="A1 road (Great Britain)">A1 road</a>, which mirrors the East Coast Main Line's course.  In County Durham this road is of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motorway" title="Motorway">motorway</a> standard and is known as the <a href="/wiki/A1(M)_motorway" title="A1(M) motorway">A1(M)</a>, and in March 2018 a section of A1(M) was opened between <a href="/wiki/Barton,_North_Yorkshire" title="Barton, North Yorkshire">Barton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leeming_Bar" title="Leeming Bar">Leeming Bar</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Yorkshire" title="North Yorkshire">North Yorkshire</a> providing a continuous link to the motorway network in the rest of England. However, the A1 is still an all-purpose road in Tyne and Wear and Northumberland, and is controversially still single carriageway north of <a href="/wiki/Morpeth,_Northumberland" title="Morpeth, Northumberland">Morpeth</a>, despite being the main trunk route connecting <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a> and Newcastle-upon-Tyne and having a terrible safety record.
</p><p>A second north-south dual carriageway link is provided by the <a href="/wiki/A19_road" title="A19 road">A19</a> which heads north from <a href="/wiki/Thirsk" title="Thirsk">Thirsk</a> (accessed via the A168 from the A1(M) at Dishforth), serving <a href="/wiki/Teesside" title="Teesside">Teesside</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peterlee" title="Peterlee">Peterlee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunderland" title="Sunderland">Sunderland</a> before heading through the <a href="/wiki/Tyne_Tunnel" title="Tyne Tunnel">Tyne Tunnel</a> to meet the A1 at <a href="/wiki/Seaton_Burn" title="Seaton Burn">Seaton Burn</a>. The Tyne Tunnel was opened as a single-carriageway in 1967, and an adjoining new tunnel was opened in February 2011.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/A1_(Newcastle_upon_Tyne)" title="A1 (Newcastle upon Tyne)">course of the A1 through Tyne and Wear</a> has changed twice during the late 20th century. Originally it passed through the centre of Newcastle-upon-Tyne, but in 1977 it was re-routed to the east of the city through the Tyne Tunnel. In 1993 it was rerouted again along the newly-completed Newcastle Western Bypass to the west of the city. Each of these moved resulted in many Tyneside roads being renumbered, as the A1 <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_road_numbering_scheme" title="Great Britain road numbering scheme">forms the boundary</a> between roads beginning with 1 and roads beginning with 6.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/A66_road" title="A66 road">A66</a> connects Teesside with Darlington, County Durham. The <a href="/wiki/A68_road" title="A68 road">A68</a> takes a cross-country central route over the <a href="/wiki/North_Pennines" title="North Pennines">North Pennines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cheviot_Hills" title="Cheviot Hills">Cheviot Hills</a> to Scotland, often following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_road" title="Roman road">Roman road</a> <a href="/wiki/Dere_Street" title="Dere Street">Dere Street</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/A69_road" title="A69 road">A69</a> heads west from Newcastle along the Tyne Valley to Hexham and then on to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carlisle,_Cumbria" title="Carlisle, Cumbria">Carlisle</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea">Sea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Sea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Queen_of_Scandinavia_at_Newcastle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Queen_of_Scandinavia_at_Newcastle.jpg/220px-Queen_of_Scandinavia_at_Newcastle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Queen_of_Scandinavia_at_Newcastle.jpg/330px-Queen_of_Scandinavia_at_Newcastle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Queen_of_Scandinavia_at_Newcastle.jpg/440px-Queen_of_Scandinavia_at_Newcastle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Queen_of_Scandinavia_at_Newcastle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MS_Princess_Maria" title="MS Princess Maria">Queen of Scandinavia</a> berthed at North Shields</div></div></div>
<p>The ferry terminal at <a href="/wiki/North_Shields" title="North Shields">North Shields</a> is accessed via the A187 from the Tyne Tunnel. <a href="/wiki/DFDS" title="DFDS">DFDS</a> operate two ferries a day to <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a> and, formerly, one a day on the <a href="/wiki/Stavanger" title="Stavanger">Stavanger</a> – <a href="/wiki/Haugesund" title="Haugesund">Haugesund</a> – <a href="/wiki/Bergen" title="Bergen">Bergen</a> route.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air">Air</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Air">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The two main airports are <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_Airport" title="Newcastle Airport">Newcastle Airport</a>, located north of the city near <a href="/wiki/Ponteland" title="Ponteland">Ponteland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Durham_Tees_Valley_Airport" title="Durham Tees Valley Airport">Durham Tees Valley Airport</a>, located east of Darlington.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_North_Air_Ambulance_G-GNAA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Great_North_Air_Ambulance_G-GNAA.jpg/220px-Great_North_Air_Ambulance_G-GNAA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Great_North_Air_Ambulance_G-GNAA.jpg/330px-Great_North_Air_Ambulance_G-GNAA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Great_North_Air_Ambulance_G-GNAA.jpg/440px-Great_North_Air_Ambulance_G-GNAA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_North_Air_Ambulance_G-GNAA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Great_North_Air_Ambulance" title="Great North Air Ambulance">Great North Air Ambulance</a></div></div></div>
<p>The region's population is served by a charitable service known as the <a href="/wiki/Great_North_Air_Ambulance" title="Great North Air Ambulance">Great North Air Ambulance</a> for those who need rapid transfer to a hospital or medical assistance in difficult or remote locations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport_policy">Transport policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Transport policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Long term planning for transport in the region has involved the development of sub regional strategies. This planning also needs to take into account region wide transport schemes such as those carried out by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highways_Agency" title="Highways Agency">Highways Agency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Network_Rail" title="Network Rail">Network Rail</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> These activities in the United Kingdom now fall into the remit of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Local_enterprise_partnerships" title="Local enterprise partnerships">Local Enterprise Partnerships</a> (LEPs) which in the Northeast of England are <a class="external text" href="https://www.teesvalleyunlimited.gov.uk/" rel="nofollow">Tees valley Unlimited</a> and the <a class="external text" href="http://www.nelep.co.uk/" rel="nofollow">North East LEP</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Bodies such as the Northeast Chamber of Commerce (NECC) and the Institution of Civil Engineers (ICE) are providers of significant practical insights to policy makers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>Within the region the local transport authorities plan for the future by producing <a href="/wiki/Local_transport_plan" title="Local transport plan">Local Transport Plans (LTP)</a> which outline their strategies, policies and implementation programmes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> The most recent LTP is that for the period 2006–11. In the North East region the following transport authorities have published their LTP online: <a href="/wiki/Darlington" title="Darlington">Darlington</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DaLTP_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-DaLTP-163">[163]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Durham,_England" title="Durham, England">Durham</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DuLTP_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-DuLTP-164">[164]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hartlepool" title="Hartlepool">Hartlepool</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaLTP_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-HaLTP-165">[165]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough" title="Middlesbrough">Middlesbrough</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MdLTP_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-MdLTP-166">[166]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Northumberland" title="Northumberland">Northumberland</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NhLTP_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-NhLTP-167">[167]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Redcar_and_Cleveland" title="Redcar and Cleveland">Redcar and Cleveland</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R&amp;CLTP_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-R&amp;CLTP-168">[168]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stockton-on-Tees" title="Stockton-on-Tees">Stockton-on-Tees</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SoTLTP_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-SoTLTP-169">[169]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tyne_and_Wear" title="Tyne and Wear">Tyne and Wear</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LTP_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-LTP-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_data">Economic data</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Economic data">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>See also the earlier sections on NE Economy and Businesses.
</p><p>The latest statistical report from the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">Office for National Statistics</a> comparing the Northeast of England to other regions of the United Kingdom,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> dated May 2012, states:
</p>
<ul><li>The North East has the highest value of goods exports relative to the size of its economy, the highest percentage employed in the public sector and lowest gross household income per head of the English regions.</li></ul>
<ul><li>The North East's exports of goods, expressed as a percentage of gross value added (GVA), were the highest of all the English regions at 29% in 2010, compared with the UK average of 20%. Over half the region's goods exports were to the EU (55%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li>A quarter of employed people in the region worked in the public sector in Q4 2011 (24.6%), the highest proportion among the English regions, down from 26.9% in Q4 2009. In 2010, at local authority level the highest shares of public sector employee jobs were to be found in Newcastle upon Tyne and Middlesbrough (both over 33% of all employee jobs).</li></ul>
<ul><li>Gross disposable household income (GDHI) of residents in the North East, at £13,300 per head in 2010, was 15% below the UK average and the lowest of the English regions. It ranged from £12,400 in Sunderland to £16,090 in Northumberland.</li></ul>
<ul><li>The North East region contributed 3% of the UK's <a href="/wiki/Gross_value_added" title="Gross value added">GVA</a>. The region's headline GVA was £41.0 billion in 2010. The latest subregional data (2009) show that Tyneside generated 37% of the region's GVA at £14.6 billion.</li></ul>
<ul><li>In 2009 manufacturing industries generated 14% of the region's total GVA, which is the largest industry contribution for the region.</li></ul>
<ul><li>Productivity in 2010 (measured by GVA per hour worked) was 88% of the UK rate: one of the lowest of the English regions. Within the region, Northumberland's productivity was the third lowest in England at 75% of the UK rate in 2009.</li></ul>
<ul><li>The region's employment rate was the lowest in England at 66.2% for Q4 2011. The latest subregional data for the year ending September 2011 show that North Tyneside had the highest employment rate at 72.6%.</li></ul>
<ul><li>The North East had the highest rate of economic inactivity of the English regions, 25% of the population aged 16 to 64 in Q4 2011.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_schools_in_England#North_East_of_England" title="Lists of schools in England">Lists of schools in England § North East of England</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_education">Primary education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Primary education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are over 250 nursery/primary schools in the County Durham area of the north east, which range from schools with their own nursery, to schools that are either infant only or junior only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Areas such as <a href="/wiki/Gosforth" title="Gosforth">Gosforth</a> have first schools which have neither years 5 nor 6, and therefore educate children up to the age of 8 and 9.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secondary_education">Secondary education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Secondary education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hummersknott_School,_Edinburgh_Drive_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137046.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="479" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Hummersknott_School%2C_Edinburgh_Drive_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137046.jpg/220px-Hummersknott_School%2C_Edinburgh_Drive_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137046.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Hummersknott_School%2C_Edinburgh_Drive_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137046.jpg/330px-Hummersknott_School%2C_Edinburgh_Drive_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137046.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Hummersknott_School%2C_Edinburgh_Drive_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137046.jpg/440px-Hummersknott_School%2C_Edinburgh_Drive_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137046.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hummersknott_School,_Edinburgh_Drive_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137046.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hummersknott_School" title="Hummersknott School">Hummersknott School</a> near Darlington</div></div></div>
<p>The North East education system consists largely of <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_school" title="Comprehensive school">comprehensive schools</a>, but a number of private and independent schools are found in Newcastle, Sunderland, Durham, Darlington, Stockton and in particular, Northumberland.
</p><p>in 2014 a number of the regions schools were in the top 20%  of schools as measured by SSAT — the UK organisation for school improvement and collaboration — and have been awarded an SSAT Educational Outcomes Award recognising their successes. These schools are Burnside Business &amp; Enterprise College, in Newcastle, Castle View Enterprise Academy, in Sunderland, Emmanuel College, in Gateshead, Greenfield Community College, in Newton Aycliffe, Northumberland Church of England Academy, in Ashington and The North Durham Academy in Stanley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> The awards, recognised high attainment and outstanding continuous improvement.
</p><p>The schools in the top 20% for high attainment in GCSE exams were Burnside Business and Enterprise College and Northumberland Church of England Academy
</p><p>The region's secondary school attendance is the lowest in England at around 125,000, with the next lowest in the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands" title="East Midlands">East Midlands</a>. <a href="/wiki/Truancy" title="Truancy">Truancy</a> at its schools is a mixed picture. It has the second highest overall rate for urban areas, after <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_and_the_Humber" title="Yorkshire and the Humber">Yorkshire and the Humber</a>, but the lowest rate in England in its rural areas. Middlesbrough has the region's highest rate with 7.2% persistent truants, which is the second highest rate in England after Manchester (7.3%). Next is Newcastle upon Tyne, with 6.4%, then the former district of <a href="/wiki/Wansbeck" title="Wansbeck">Wansbeck</a>, with 6.3%
</p><p>At <a href="/wiki/General_Certificate_of_Secondary_Education" title="General Certificate of Secondary Education">General Certificate of Secondary Education</a> (GCSE) level, the region's performance is similar to that of other largely urban areas, although its results are generally below the national average. Middlesbrough tends to perform the worst, and to produce results well below the national average. <a href="/wiki/Local_education_authority" title="Local education authority">Local education authorities</a> (LEAs) in the North East have improved at GCSE in recent years. North Tyneside performed the best in 2011, followed by Gateshead, Northumberland, and Darlington. No LEA in the North-East was above the 2011 national GCSE average. South Tyneside was the lowest for the <a href="/wiki/English_Baccalaureate" title="English Baccalaureate">English Baccalaureate</a>, followed by Middlesbrough and Hartlepool. Gateshead and North Tyneside were the highest, and Gateshead was the only LEA above the national average for this measure.
</p><p>The region's <a href="/wiki/Parochial_school" title="Parochial school">parochial schools</a> tend to perform better at GCSE. These include <a href="/wiki/St_Thomas_More_Catholic_School,_Blaydon" title="St Thomas More Catholic School, Blaydon">St Thomas More Catholic School</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blaydon" title="Blaydon">Blaydon</a>, the selective independent state school <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emmanuel_City_Technology_College" title="Emmanuel City Technology College">Emmanuel City Technology College</a> in Gateshead, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=All_Saints_C_of_E_School&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="All Saints C of E School (page does not exist)">All Saints C of E School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ingleby_Barwick" title="Ingleby Barwick">Ingleby Barwick</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_Martyrs_School_and_Sixth_Form_College" title="English Martyrs School and Sixth Form College">English Martyrs School and Sixth Form College</a> in Hartlepool, <a href="/wiki/St_Bede%27s_Catholic_School_and_Sixth_Form_College" title="St Bede's Catholic School and Sixth Form College">St Bede's Catholic School and Sixth Form College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lanchester,_County_Durham" title="Lanchester, County Durham">Lanchester, County Durham</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carmel_RC_Technology_College" title="Carmel RC Technology College">Carmel RC Technology College</a> in Darlington. Other regional schools that perform well include <a href="/wiki/Whitley_Bay_High_School" title="Whitley Bay High School">Whitley Bay High School</a> the <a href="/wiki/Macmillan_Academy" title="Macmillan Academy">Macmillan Academy</a> in Middlesbrough, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Park_View_School_(Chester-le-Street)" title="Park View School (Chester-le-Street)">Park View School</a> in Chester-le Street, and the <a href="/wiki/Hurworth_School" title="Hurworth School">Hurworth School</a> near Darlington. Many area schools do not have a sixth form, especially in Teesside, Sunderland and South Tyneside, but all Northumberland schools have a sixth-form along with a <a href="/wiki/Three-tier_education" title="Three-tier education">three-tier system</a> of education. Middlesbrough and Newcastle have the most students who pass no GCSEs.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:QESFC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="450" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/QESFC.jpg/220px-QESFC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/QESFC.jpg/330px-QESFC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/QESFC.jpg/440px-QESFC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:QESFC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_Sixth_Form_College" title="Queen Elizabeth Sixth Form College">QE Sixth Form College</a> in Darlington</div></div></div>
<p>North East LEAs at A-level are improving, but produce results below those of other areas of England. Sunderland performed the best in 2011, with consistently good results, followed by Hartlepool and Darlington, which are above the national average, and unrepresentative of most areas in the North East. Darlington's <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_Sixth_Form_College" title="Queen Elizabeth Sixth Form College">Queen Elizabeth Sixth Form College</a> is one of the highest-rated colleges in England. The area's <a href="/wiki/Catholic_school" title="Catholic school">Catholic schools</a> all do reasonably well at A level. Stockton-on-Tees, Redcar, and Cleveland and Newcastle were also above England's average. Newcastle does significantly better at A-level than at GCSE, with consistent improvement, while Gateshead does much worse at A-level than GCSE, and produced the second lowest A-level results in the region in 2009. The worst results at A-level were from Middlesbrough, Durham, South Tyneside and Gateshead. South Tyneside had in previous years been consistently the region's worst performing LEA at A-level.
</p><p>The independent and private schools in the area perform highly. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dame_Allan%27s_Schools" title="Dame Allan's Schools">Dame Allan's Schools</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Grammar_School,_Newcastle" title="Royal Grammar School, Newcastle">Royal Grammar School</a> (NRGS), <a href="/wiki/Barnard_Castle_School" title="Barnard Castle School">Barnard Castle School</a> and <a href="/wiki/Durham_School" title="Durham School">Durham School</a> are all members of the prestigious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Headmasters%27_and_Headmistresses%27_Conference" title="The Headmasters' and Headmistresses' Conference">The Headmasters' and Headmistresses' Conference</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Newcastle_High_School" title="Central Newcastle High School">Central Newcastle High School</a> and the Royal Grammar School were named as among the nation's top 100 independent schools in 2006. Durham School is considered to be one of the oldest schools in the UK, and its Old Boys were the founding members of the original <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_Falcons" title="Newcastle Falcons">Newcastle Falcons</a> Rugby club. <a href="/wiki/Mowden_Hall_School" title="Mowden Hall School">Mowden Hall School</a>, a selective day and boarding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preparatory_school_(UK)" title="Preparatory school (UK)">prep school</a> in Northumberland, is another well-established and reputable independent school. The private schools out-perform the state schools in the urban areas.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the region, school children from Northumberland are most likely to attend university, followed by Stockton on Tees and North Tyneside.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_education">Further education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Further education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are sixteen further education colleges in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> The main such colleges are <a href="/wiki/East_Durham_College" title="East Durham College">East Durham College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_College" title="Newcastle College">Newcastle College</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_College_Durham" title="New College Durham">New College Durham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Darlington_College" title="Darlington College">Darlington College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gateshead_College" title="Gateshead College">Gateshead College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bishop_Auckland_College" title="Bishop Auckland College">Bishop Auckland College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stockton_Riverside_College" title="Stockton Riverside College">Stockton Riverside College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_Sixth_Form_College" title="Queen Elizabeth Sixth Form College">Queen Elizabeth Sixth Form College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hartlepool_College_of_Further_Education" title="Hartlepool College of Further Education">Hartlepool College of Further Education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middlesbrough_College" title="Middlesbrough College">Middlesbrough College</a>, Cleveland College of Art and Design and <a href="/wiki/Sunderland_College" title="Sunderland College">Sunderland College</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:University_of_Newcastle_Upon_Tyne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/University_of_Newcastle_Upon_Tyne.jpg/220px-University_of_Newcastle_Upon_Tyne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/University_of_Newcastle_Upon_Tyne.jpg/330px-University_of_Newcastle_Upon_Tyne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/University_of_Newcastle_Upon_Tyne.jpg/440px-University_of_Newcastle_Upon_Tyne.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:University_of_Newcastle_Upon_Tyne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newcastle University</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tertiary_education">Tertiary education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Tertiary education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Higher_education" title="Higher education">higher education</a> level the North East contains a number of universities. This includes <a href="/wiki/Durham_University" title="Durham University">Durham University</a>, which is the third oldest in England and is often ranked among the ten leading UK universities, <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_University" title="Newcastle University">Newcastle University</a>, a member of the <a href="/wiki/Russell_Group" title="Russell Group">Russell Group</a>, and the newer universities of <a href="/wiki/Northumbria_University" title="Northumbria University">Northumbria University</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sunderland" title="University of Sunderland">University of Sunderland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teesside_University" title="Teesside University">Teesside University</a>, which was voted Best University in the United Kingdom at the 2009 Times Higher Education awards.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Queen%27s_Campus_Stockton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10982.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Queen%27s_Campus_Stockton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10982.jpg/220px-Queen%27s_Campus_Stockton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10982.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Queen%27s_Campus_Stockton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10982.jpg/330px-Queen%27s_Campus_Stockton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10982.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Queen%27s_Campus_Stockton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10982.jpg/440px-Queen%27s_Campus_Stockton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10982.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Queen%27s_Campus_Stockton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10982.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Durham University, Queen's Campus, Stockton-on-Tees</div></div></div>
<p>Over 50% of the region's students come from the region, and around 35% are from other regions. At first degree level, around 55% are from the North of England, and about 30% are from the North East. More students come from elsewhere than leave the North East for other regions. Of students native to the region, 80% study in the North of England, with <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_and_the_Humber" title="Yorkshire and the Humber">Yorkshire and the Humber</a> more popular than the <a href="/wiki/North_West_England" title="North West England">North West</a>, and around 55% study in the North East. The region has a higher proportion of students from so-called low participation neighbourhoods, as compared to elsewhere in England. Durham University has the least from these neighbourhoods. Northumbria University has the most students, followed by Teesside University. 
</p><p>Almost 60% of graduates stay in the region, while 10% go to Yorkshire and another 10% go to London.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Reliable source needed for the whole sentence (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Both areas are accessible via the <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_Main_Line" title="East Coast Main Line">East Coast Main Line</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_media">Local media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_East_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Local media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BBC_Newcastle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/BBC_Newcastle.jpg/220px-BBC_Newcastle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/BBC_Newcastle.jpg/330px-BBC_Newcastle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/BBC_Newcastle.jpg/440px-BBC_Newcastle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BBC_Newcastle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/BBC_North_East_and_Cumbria" title="BBC North East and Cumbria">BBC</a> building in north Newcastle</div></div></div>
<p>Local media include:
</p><p>Regional television is provided by the <a href="/wiki/BBC_North_East_and_Cumbria" title="BBC North East and Cumbria">BBC North East and Cumbria</a>, which broadcasts the regional evening <i><a href="/wiki/BBC_Look_North_(North_East_and_Cumbria)" title="BBC Look North (North East and Cumbria)">Look North</a></i> programme from <a href="/wiki/Spital_Tongues" title="Spital Tongues">Spital Tongues</a> in Newcastle. Its commercial rival, <a href="/wiki/ITV_Tyne_Tees_%26_Border" title="ITV Tyne Tees &amp; Border">ITV Tyne Tees &amp; Border</a>, broadcasts the evening programme <i><a href="/wiki/ITV_News_Tyne_Tees" title="ITV News Tyne Tees">ITV News Tyne Tees</a></i> from Gateshead.
</p><p>BBC Radios <a href="/wiki/BBC_Newcastle" title="BBC Newcastle">Newcastle</a> and <a href="/wiki/BBC_Tees" title="BBC Tees">Tees</a>. National radio comes from <a href="/wiki/Bilsdale_transmitting_station" title="Bilsdale transmitting station">Bilsdale</a> on the <a href="/wiki/North_York_Moors" title="North York Moors">North York Moors</a> for Teesside, <a href="/wiki/Pontop_Pike_transmitting_station" title="Pontop Pike transmitting station">Pontop Pike</a> in County Durham for Tyne and Wear, and <a href="/wiki/Chatton_transmitting_station" title="Chatton transmitting station">Chatton</a> near <a href="/wiki/Wooler" title="Wooler">Wooler</a> for Northumberland. These transmitters are also the main television transmitters. Commercial radio stations such as <a href="/wiki/Metro_Radio" title="Metro Radio">Metro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heart_North_East" title="Heart North East">Heart</a> (formally Real radio), <a href="/wiki/Capital_North_East" title="Capital North East">Capital</a> (formerly Galaxy), <a href="/wiki/TFM_(radio)" title="TFM (radio)">TFM</a>, <a href="/wiki/TFM_2" title="TFM 2">TFM 2</a> (formally called Magic), <a href="/wiki/Sun_FM" title="Sun FM">Sun FM</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rathergood_Radio" title="Rathergood Radio">Rathergood Radio</a> (formally Star Radio). <a href="/wiki/Digital_radio_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Digital radio in the United Kingdom">Digital radio</a> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Bauer_Radio" title="Bauer Radio">Bauer Tyne &amp; Wear</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bauer_Radio" title="Bauer Radio">Bauer Teesside</a> multiplexes.There is also a variety of community radio stations such as <a href="/wiki/NE1fm" title="NE1fm">NE1fm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radio_Hartlepool" title="Radio Hartlepool">Radio Hartlepool</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spark_FM" title="Spark FM">Spark FM</a>.
</p><p>Regional newspapers include the <i><a href="/wiki/Evening_Chronicle" title="Evening Chronicle">Evening Chronicle</a>, </i><a href="/wiki/Sunderland_Echo" title="Sunderland Echo">Sunderland Echo</a><i> , <a href="/wiki/The_Journal_(newspaper)" title="The Journal (newspaper)">The Journal</a>,</i> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evening_Gazette_(Teesside)" title="Evening Gazette (Teesside)">Evening Gazette</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Shields_Gazette" title="Shields Gazette">Shields Gazette</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hartlepool_Mail" title="Hartlepool Mail">Hartlepool Mail</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Northern_Echo" title="The Northern Echo">The Northern Echo</a></i> and the <i>Darlington and Stockton Times</i>. There are also free publications such as <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Ferryhill_Chapter&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Ferryhill Chapter (page does not exist)">The Ferryhill Chapter</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bishop_Press&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bishop Press (page does not exist)">Bishop Press</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hartlepool_Post" title="The Hartlepool Post">The Hartlepool Post</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shildon_Town_Crier&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shildon Town Crier (page does not exist)">Shildon Town Crier</a></i>.
</p>
